0783225849|5.0|Alfred Hitchcock was at the top of his craft in the late 50's, with his TV show, and having released both Vertigo and N by NW. Many say he set out to one-up the French suspense film Diabolique, for which he failed to secure the rights. I believe he not only surpassed Les Diaboliques but created one of the most enduring pieces of art in all American cinema. The man was an artist, and his canvas was brimming with richness here.  From its opening shot through a motel window, Psycho is a tense study of adults misbehaving which never lets up until the final frame. Marion Crane, after a lunchtime tryst, steals $40,000 from her employer's client, which she is supposed to deposit into the bank, but she flees. Viewers are immersed into her character and kept in suspense as to what she'll do with the loot, where she will go once she decides to keep it. On the run, she is seen by the client, interrogated by a policeman while napping on the roadside, swaps her car for another, and stumbles upon an isolated motel in a rainstorm. Up to this point, Hitch has painted images with his camera, posed questions rarely asked before 1960 in a film, and shown one of the lamdmark images in celluloid: the house on the hill above the Bates Motel.  Inside the house, one of the most richly developed, sympathetic villains in memory, Norman Bates, is introduced. The audience's ability to relate to this friendly yet reclusive taxadermist compells us to keep watching--as his vouyerism compells him. Here, Hitchcock uses stuffed birds not only to suggest Norman's self imposed imprisonment but to prepare audiences for his upscoming film, The Birds.  In the meantime, the plot never slows, as a concerned sister and the victim's love interest, flanked by a detective, trace her to the motel. Here, among psychologial dialogue, suggestive character interplay, and an unrelenting atmosphere, we are shown more landmarks: a protagonist's death less than 2/3 into the film, a murder in a shower, the spiraling ofblood (chocolate syrup) down the shower drain which mirrors the protagonist's dead eye, a falling-backward-down-a-staircase murder scene, and the first ever shot of a toilet in a feature film...all original and bold ideas.  This film has palpable atmosphere, great dialogue without being overdone, a razor sharp script, phenomenal directing and score, very good acting, and crisp cinematography. We are shown all we need to see--but never too much--through dazzling camera angles, ominous lighting and eerie shadows. Ultimately, the woman-within-a-man who is responsible for the carnage is one we can sympathize with, even root for, and that keeps us from ever looking away. In fact, I come back. I've come back to this film over and over again, and its power never fades.  This is Hitchcock's high water mark. First rate film, and my all time favorite.

Now it seems there are novels, and there are graphic novels.<br /><br />Not here. This is a seamless match between the evocative prose and these haunting windows into the story. B001RCD2DW|5.0|Im sure you dont want to read yet another wall of text about the pros and cons of these phones or detailed review of audio characteristics, there are plenty here. So i am going to focus on things you should know before you purchase these \\"cans\\"    If you are reading reviews on amazon, chances are you dont know much about high end head phones (no offense intended) A good idea before your purchase would be to head over to \\"head-fi.org\\" and read threads on the forums to see if these are for you or if there is something more suitable. pay attention more to what people say about the characteristics of the headphones, not if they like / dislike them. sound is a personal thing and everyone's taste is different.    failing that, points to note are    1. If paired with high end, or better yet, lower cost but synergistic gear these punch well above their price. But they are very picky about what you put with them. do research about what amp and dac you pair them with. put the wrong gear with them and they will sound very poor, chose well and they can be astronomically good for the price.     2. These NEED good amplification, dont think you will get away with running them off a headphone jack as even if you can get enough volume out of them they will sound no better than a $50 set of cans. Some people drive these with headphone amps costing more than $1500 for example. General consensus though is that a Fiio E9 will run these well enough for most, so factor in that cost of that. A E7 DAC may be a good idea too if your sound card is poor.     3. These are open backed head phones. they have literally NO sound insulation AT ALL, nor are they meant to. At normal listening volume they can be heard from several meters away. Take these out in public and everyone will hate you. These are a set for home.     4. High quality audio is required to make the most out of these. MP3s still sound OK, but play a flac and a MP3 of the same song back to back and the Flac has noticeably more detail and depth.     5. Burn in makes a BIG difference. Mine have about 100 hours on them now and sound much better than originally when they were new. they develop more bass and become less sterile over time, but keep in mind that these will always be a come what clinical sounding can, with emphasis on sound stage and accuracy over fun.     I recently bought a set of these from amazon, and i love them i am picking up things in songs that for my whole life i have been missing. pick up a affordable ( <$200) amp that works well with them (again, read up on what works), suits your tastes (amps change the sound and tone of headphones somewhat) and almost anyone will be happy with these assuming they dont expect them to be something they are not.

B004HO6I4M|3.0|20th Century Fox has given us the likes of Fantastic Mr. Fox, Horton Hears a Who, and, of course, the Ice Age films as far as animated films go. While I did thoroughly enjoy both Fantastic Mr. Fox and Horton Hears a Who, both films were adapted from novels. Despite usually having stunning animation, original animated films created by 20th Century Fox like Ice Age lack something Pixar films such as Up, WALL-E, Ratatouille, and the Toy Story films utilize in the best of ways. Unfortunately, Rio, despite its best efforts, seems to continue this trend.<br /><br />Rio manages to make its visuals its most cherished attribute. The 3-D animated adventure is intensely rich with spectacular color as every frame of the film is filled with nothing but the most bright and brilliant hues. Calling the film eye candy, in this case, would be nothing more than a compliment for a film like this. It is definitely the most vivid animated film in recent memory. With films these days, everything is so focused on being dark and serious while bright colors are replaced with various shades of gray and gloomy shadows. Rio's dynamic use of color practically makes it a happy film in tone right off the bat and is a welcome change of pace.<br /><br />However, everything else about the film either left something to be desired or was executed improperly. The finale seems to be wrapped up so quickly. It's been building to this moment right here for an hour and a half. For everything to culminate so hastily followed by an even faster epilogue made it all feel very rushed. Rio also doesn't take the necessary steps to build foundation into your emotions either. Every time it touches on a heart wrenching moment for one of the talking animals we've grown attached to, it seems like it jumps back to silly shenanigans too quickly for it to make any sort of impact on you. Then there's the humor of the film that's really touch and go. It'll probably be fun for most kids, but adults weren't the ones filling the theater with uproarious laughter. The animated film's content is more cute or charming than hilarious or groundbreaking.<br /><br />Most of the voices fit the characters really well. Blu is Jesse Eisenberg and vice versa, George Lopez's humor just drips from Rafael's feathers, and Tracy Morgan fits Luiz like a glove. However, Jemaine Clement's voice work for Nigel may have stolen the show. His song is the most enjoyable on the soundtrack and the Nigel character is just one that you love to hate. Also, pretty much any scene involving the thieving marmosets is gold. Those marmosets are to Rio what the penguins are to Madagascar.<br /><br />Rio is both fun and beautifully animated, but the film seems to lack the heart that makes Pixar films so great. 20th Century Fox delivers a film that looks just as good as the best Pixar films, but is only able to scratch the surface of your emotions when it matters most. The humor included in the film seems to fall flat more often than not, as well. It'll make you smirk at times, but nothing is really laugh out loud funny. In the end, Rio is still an amusing animated adventure that's suitable for the entire family but adult viewers may walk away slightly disappointed.proarious laughter. All in all, the musicianship and production (Norman Smith) are outstanding, and every song is a wonder worth pondering. Without doubt, the interplay between Wright's keyboards and Barrett's guitar is what drives the whole show.  The two were of one mindset, and on this album they soared as musical counterparts.<br /><br />This 2007 remastered edition includes both mono and stereo mixes. I have tried to compare them to each other, but haven't quite made up my mind which I prefer. Either that or my stereo is not good enough to tell the difference? The intro is a full frontal attack. It's the finest visual representation of Syd Barrett available. Even in black and white, Syd waves his wand, and the magic happens. 0393338827|5.0|Michael Lewis is just a great, great writer. \\"Liar's Poker\\" is a classic read for all traders. \\"Moneyball\\" was one of the best books I have read in years. Lewis' Vanity Fair articles on Iceland, Greece and Ireland were all must reads.<br /><br />When it came to \\"The Big Short,\\" though, I found myself hesitant to read it. Not for lack of interest, but because I was tapped out on financial crisis books at the time it was published.<br /><br />After \\"Too Big To Fail\\" by Sorkin, \\"The Greatest Trade Ever\\" by Zuckerman, \\"The End of Wall Street\\" by Lowenstein, and a stack of other materials eight inches high, I was sick of reading about the subprime crisis and its aftermath.<br /><br />\\"The Big Short\\" is made all the better, though, by revisiting the topic a year or two down the road. As Leonardo da Vinci once said: \\"Every now and then go away, have a little relaxation, for when you come back your judgment will be surer. Go some distance away, because the work appears smaller and more of it can be taken in.\\"<br /><br />The da Vinci advice also holds true for major market events.<br /><br />Thumbing through \\"The Big Short\\" in a bookstore prior to a plane trip, I was immediately drawn in by the color and the profiles. It's the little details, plus the great explanations and laugh-out-loud moments, that make Lewis so good.<br /><br />The book tells the story of the subprime crisis through a handful of worthy characters (who all made a killing in the collapse): Steve Eisman, the foul-mouthed iconoclast disgusted by Wall Street; Michael Burry, the one-eyed doctor with Asperger's Syndrome; Greg Lippman, the bond trader with Fonzie sideburns who talked of ripping people's eyeballs out; Charlie Ledley and Jamie Mai, two goofballs who started out in a Berkeley garage with a Charles Schwab account, and so on.<br /><br />Thinking about the subprime crisis with the benefit of da Vinci's distance, it struck me anew how Darwinian and predatory the whole system is. One constantly has to ask, Cui Bono: \\"Who benefits?\\" And Ubi Est Mea: \\"Where's mine?\\" One of Eisman's traders was constantly obsessed with how the party on the other side might screw him (though \\"screw\\" was not the word used). That is probably a good attitude to have on Wall Street.<br /><br />Lewis also does a good job of explaining why the subprime disaster was so huge. What Goldman and the other i-banks did was diabolically ingenious: When it became obvious there were not enough actual mortgages to go around, they simply created bets on top of bets (via complicated derivative products) for the purpose of fulfilling speculative demand.<br /><br />In this the banks were like bookies making a point spread on the Super Bowl -- except this wagering pool was 1,000 times bigger. Through vehicles like \\"CDOs squared,\\" they took on hundreds of billions in bets with no direct market function, enjoying the middleman's cut.<br /><br />On one side, bears like Eisman, Burry, Lippmann and Paulson sought to short every bad mortgage they could find. On the other side, happy-go-lucky bulls like Wing Chau sought to buy every crappy mortgage product they could find, taking in ridiculous fees for \\"managing\\" these pools of toxic high yield assets on behalf of brain-dead institutional investors. (Like the \\"stupid Germans,\\" as Lewis repeatedly references them.)<br /><br />In many ways, Wall Street is a giant casino. While activity on Wall Street is supposed to be productive and purposeful, much of it is pure gambling. And with side bets hundreds of times bigger than the actual market, in exotic subprime derivatives the casino operators had found a loophole bigger than the St. Louis arch.<br /><br />The trouble, though, is that the prey was too stupid this time. For a predator-prey relationship to hold up over the long term, there has to be a sustainable ecology balance. The \\"dumb money\\" cannot be so dumb as to completely incinerate itself. The actions of AIG and the ratings agencies were just so fantasticallymoronic, however, that the sharks simply could not help gorging to the point of bursting.<br /><br />And then, in the height of the feeding frenzy, the predators got dumb too. In holding too much product on the books, the i-banks were getting \\"high on their own supply\\" as Tony Montana might phrase it. The final result was pure insanity, and the foundation-shaking disaster that followed.<br /><br />Reviewing this book in 2011, I am reminded (yet again) of just how insane Wall Street can be. \\"Mr. Market,\\" as  famously described by Benjamin Graham, is often portrayed as a basically logical guy. But the reality is a bit different. In truth, Mr. Market is a cross between Gordon Gekko and Forrest Gump. Sometimes very savvy, sometimes insatiably greedy, he can also be so intensely thick-headed as to boggle the mind. That is why the market has a distinctly non-efficient habit of driving itself off a cliff every so often. This happens over and over again.<br /><br />With the detailed chronicling of the subcrime crisis that \\"The Big Short\\" and other books like it provide, one would hope the academics would wake up. Up until now, academia has been on the Forrest Gump side of the equation, pushing perfect-world theories so obtuse as to make a trader's eyes cross. The reality of the marketplace is overwhelmingly human, with all the gullibility and deceit and rampant self-interest that such a reality entails. To the degree we collectively forget that, the stage is set for more disaster.d then, in the height of the feeding frenzy, the predators got dumb too. In holding too much product on the books, the i-banks were getting \\"high on their own supply\\" as Tony Montana might phrase it. The final result was pure insanity, and the foundation-shaking disaster that followed. Sometimes very savvy, sometimes insatiably greedy, he can also be so intensely thick-headed as to boggle the mind. That is why the market has a distinctly non-efficient habit of driving itself off a cliff every so often. This happens over and over again. In some ways he's a sleazy guy, representing biker gangs, bribing clerks for info etc, but he's also got a kind heart for his daughter, his ex and his friend Frank (Bill Macy, a private investigator).  I think it's good role for McConaughey, and like a lot of other Hollywood \\"pretty boys\\" (e.g. Tom Cruise, Leonardo DiCaprio) his acting gets better as he ages and plays more serious parts.  If you'd ever gotten accused of a crime, his Mickey Haller character would be the guy you'd hire to battle the system!    Haller comes upon a case of a snobby rich young man (Ryan Phillippe) who is accused of a nasty assault on a prostitute, and takes over the case from the family's clueless \\"blue-blood\\" lawyer.  Then the story gets intriguing, as you wonder if the rich kid is \\"playing\\" Haller.  Then you realize that he is, big time, especially when Macy gets killed, and Haller seems like he's in a deep hole that's impossible to get out of.  It's when he's in this impossible-seeming situation that McConaughey's acting skills come out.  Is he a cool lawyer with the whole world at his feet, or a lonely, angry, divorced drunk who is barely clinging on without losing it?  It's this time of desparation & suffering that gives the movie a little more depth than your typical formulaic film noir.    All Haller has to do is lose the case and the rich snob will be jailed for a long, long time.  But he's set on winning the case for his client and simultaneously getting his client booked for murder. How he does this is a little too slick for me to believe, but I admit it's good escapism.  Also, as many viewers say, the movie has well acted characters. It's because it is a total warping of reality.  This movie, while it PURPORTS to be realistic, is total BS reality-wise.  A sleazy defense lawyer who is amoral and works the system for money, is the hero?  A white, handsome, upper-class, Ferrari-driving real-estate salesman and his well-dressed successful Mother are evil Bad Guys, like the Joker and the Riddler?  Biker gangs are other heros who are on the side of the hero?  Of course, we HAVE TO BE PC.  SO ... can't have Blacks or Hispanics, who are 95% of the gang population in LA, be the criminals!  No!  The gang-looking latino who was put in jail for murder, was framed!  He even says \\"My Mom is going to die when she knows I'm going to jail!\\"  He says it so well, you think you're going to cry!  Forget about all those other gangbangers who aren't innocent (like the guys who beat the Giants fan into a coma), this movie tells you that you just can't generalize!      So the movie (& I assume the book) depicts a flipped reality in which the population that is probably responsible for <1% of the violent crime in the US (rich, white, USC-educated real-estate selling golfers, AND their mothers) are the evil-doers.  And everyone else in the non-upper-class world: prostitutes, ex-hippies, minorities, bikers, sleaze-bags, are just fine ... i.e. they're GOOD PEOPLE.   Who we should really hate are those rich golfing bastards, not only because we're jealous of their money, and they're not taxed enough of course, but they are evil serial killers to boot and guarded by their homicidal 65 year-old Armani-clothed mothers.    In other words, what passes for a thriller on the surface is also very manipulative class warfare and PC propaganda.  But I guess it's worth a few bucks for some temporary escapism.

0312600844|4.0|Most present-day American archetypes will see a reflection of themselves somewhere in Jonathan Franzen's \\"Freedom.\\"<br /><br />And they will most likely cringe.<br /><br />The author may or may not be the second coming of the greatest American novelist, but he is definitely a good, and most American novelist.<br /><br />And for sure, he forgoes a place in the classical canon with his frequent pop references and appeals to the current national sensibilities, but Franzen's got a few things to say about the people of the United States and gosh darn if he isn't going to say them.<br /><br />It reportedly took the author 10 years to write \\"Freedom,\\" but he was not simply grooming something that was drafted in 2002. He followed the nation's progress, or lack of progress as he seems to suggest, growing his story right up until the financial crisis of 2008.<br /><br />\\"Freedom\\" represents the triumph of a kind of literary reporting. Franzen's people swim in the zeitgeist the way we all do, like it or not.<br /><br />The novel charts a Midwestern family of four's wade through the 1970s all the way to the aforementioned sub-prime market meltdown with a keen eye on what makes an American throughout the epoch under examination.<br /><br />This family of his mind's creation, the Berglunds, with the help of their antecedents, siblings and offspring, swim in the current of contemporary events without the author ever seeming to stretch things to fit his scheme.<br /><br />He comments on our ugly national mood, growing intolerance, gaping inequalities, corruptions, perversion and decadence with irrefutable accuracy, sparing none, right. left, straight, gay, Christian, secular, blue or red.<br /><br />There is a density to the prose. Some have said the author uses too many words, but if that is the case, it is rarely in useless or neurotic digression. The action moves along all the while employing the kind techniques that separate finer literature from a good potboiler.<br /><br />And for all the darkness and foreboding Franzen thrusts upon his ample readership, he manages to close on an optimistic note, which, too, makes him very American.<br /><br />All of it while seemingly riffing an effortless path through his own sentiments, when those in the know will understand how much more went into this fine and worthy work.d for all the darkness and foreboding Franzen thrusts upon his ample readership, he manages to close on an optimistic note, which, too, makes him very American.<br /><br />All of it while seemingly riffing an effortless path through his own sentiments, when those in the know will understand how much more went into this fine and worthy work.

0307389839|3.0|Haruki Murakami (\\"After Dark,\\" \\"Dance Dance Dance\\") took up both writing and long distance running in the early 1980s and has never turned away from either passion.  With \\"What I Talk About When I Talk About Running,\\" Murakami tries to help the reader understand just why it is that he insists on running at least one marathon every year, year after year.  Unfortunately, the story rarely gets all that interesting.    Essentially the book boils down to, Murakami likes to run long distances even though distance running is a lonely, painful pursuit.  He trains a lot, because training is important to distance running. In the middle of a race, he often hates what he's doing, but then he finishes and everything is OK.  He's getting older, so his body is slowing down.  So he tries triathlons.    That's about it, and the writing is about as flat and dull as that paragraph.  There are some interesting tidbits, such as Murakami's struggle with a painful knee and his crazy entry into a 62-mile super-marathon.  But these stories exist in a vacuum and don't really go anywhere - his knee stops hurting, and he finishes the super-marathon.  Murakami believes that both distance running and writing a novel require tremendous endurance . . . not to be too flippant, but reading this slim memoir (180 pages for the 2008 hardcover edition) felt like a slog as well.    The memoir offers little insight into Murakami's situation.  What does his wife think about his dedication to running?  Hard to say - I'm not even sure that Murakami tells us his wife's name.  Murakami writes a bit about the runner's blues, but offers far less insight into the distance runner's mind than, say, Christopher MacDougall's \\"Born to Run.\\"  Ultimately, the book has very little to offer.    Murakami must be a heck of a writer.  He writes briefly about the awards he's won and the fact that he gets to lecture in Cambridge.  But based on this slim volume, we have to assume that his other books must be terrific indeed.

0199606080|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo2B2J4IJDJM7PU]]A MIXED RECORD?<br /><br />An Appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />If you've ever had any cause to be sceptical about human rights, or rather, human rights legislation with many of its implications, whether UK-based or worldwide, then this book is for you.<br /><br />It contains a choice collection of `sceptical essays' on human rights from 24 leading human rights scholars from around the world, encompassing  not only the EU and the UK, but a number of other jurisdictions, including Australia, Canada, the United States  and more besides.<br /><br />As the editors point out, the spirit of scepticism surrounding human rights issues in the UK has been fuelled by `the apparent paradox of a Human Rights Act being introduced by a government which then proceeded to go a long way to dismantle the very rights and freedoms which the Human Rights Act was designed to protect'.<br /><br />Underlining their point, the authors cite extended police powers of arrest and search...the extension of surveillance powers of local authorities (without the necessity of a warrant)...and the increased use of CCTV and the DNA database, said to be the world's largest.  There have been other disconcerting developments in response to, ostensibly, the `war on terror', namely the indefinite detention of terrorist suspects without their being convicted, the use of control orders and the ugly suspicion of a lax attitude to torture.<br /><br />We must add here that at the time of the book's publication by OUP in July 2010, the UK's new coalition government had been in place for only about three months and, as we write in the first quarter of 2011, many of the above mentioned excesses have already been modified or dismantled altogether.<br /><br />Nevertheless, human rights continues to be a vexed issue requiring continuing vigilance and, yes, a spirit of enlightened and informed scepticism which this book exemplifies.<br /><br />The editors identify 2 kinds of scepticism here: ideological and institutional. Ideological scepticism revolves mainly around `doubts about the politically biased content of much of human rights law.'  Institutional skepticism focuses on the (very worrying) decline in recent years of the role of parliaments and the corresponding extension of the political role of the courts in interpreting and enforcing human rights legislation.<br /><br />In drawing attention to these concerns, this book has done a worthy service in adding argument and ammunition to this debate, the outcome of which will certainly affect us all.<br /><br />To aid further research -- as well as debate -- the book is meticulously foot noted and, for the convenience of practitioners, academics and students alike, there are copious tables of cases, treaties and legislation. So, the jury has gone out again with a new Bill of Rights committee, but it is a mixed record they are now reviewing.e: ideological and institutional. Having arisen and evolved from the historic need to protect investors from the consequences of business failure, liquidation, as the author points out, is `absolutely central to the UK insolvency regime and an essential branch of company and commercial law'.<br /><br />The author, Vernon Dennis, is an acknowledged expert in this often difficult and emotionally charged area, acting regularly for creditors, debtors and insolvency practitioners.  His prose is clear; his approach practical in 9 chapters with their `at a glance' guidance checklists in the appendices.<br /><br />With the reasoning behind the legislative framework highlighted throughout, the book's 200 pages provide invaluable practical advice for everyone and anyone who advises companies, directors, creditors, customers and suppliers to business who may be either undertaking or considering liquidation as a preferred insolvency procedure.<br /><br />The book covers the three forms of liquidation:  compulsory, creditors' voluntary and members' voluntary liquidation.  Although these processes are similar, the book specifically highlights and explains the differences.<br /><br />Logically structured, the book explains first the main characteristics and purpose of liquidation and offers advice on how to start the process.  The actual processes and procedures of liquidation are then examined in detail and analysis is provided on the implications of liquidation for the directors of the insolvent company and its creditors.  The final chapter deals with the various steps to be taken in ending the liquidation process.<br /><br />What makes this book impressively easy to use are the nine appendices we referred to earlier -- one for each chapter-- each outlining and summarizing by means of checklists and flowcharts, the author's succinct `at a glance' advice. Interestingly, the chart in Appendix 1 indicates that despite fluctuations, the total of corporate insolvencies in the UK rose from 12,856 in 1987 to 25,432 in 2009.<br /><br />No doubt this trend will continue, particularly if the economy remains less than buoyant - hence the need for this timely and authoritative book.<br /><br />To aid further research, there are lengthy tables of cases, statutes, statutory instruments, international statutory instruments and legislation, together with the appendices I have just mentioned, plus a useful glossary and index. 0307887960|4.0|In the wake of the new popularity of the HCG diet--where either injections or a homeopathic substitute for the HCG hormone is administered to the body which enables the consumption of a near starvation diet of 500 calories per day--I look favorably at plans like the Dukan Diet as a more sensible, long-term alternative regime for healthful living.  I have been an advocate of Jana Klauer's real food diet, \\"[[ASIN:B002KE482U How the Rich Get Thin: Park Avenue's Top Diet Doctor Reveals the Secrets to Losing Weight and Feeling Great]]\\" for over six years.  As a medical doctor who teaches her patients the art of sensible eating, she has a good grasp on what works, what is healthy and how to maintain it, despite her market introduction of a signature, albeit nasty tasting, diet drink (which, in my opinion negates the whole idea of eating real food rather than relying on a packaged gimmick.)  Klauer emphasizes the importance of calcium with each meal and whether or not this is substantiated by any scientific study or not, I have found that it works for me.<br /><br />At first glance, \\"The Dukan Diet,\\" by Dr. Pierre Dukan, a medical practitioner in France, seems to be an Atkins offshoot.  However, I hardly think this is the case as Dukan, like Klauer, suggests consuming high quality protein and little fat for optimal weight loss and health.  Shucks! No bacon to be found here. Where Klauer suggests primarily the leanest and most pure forms of protein--egg whites, non-fat dairy products (indeed, some may argue that dairy is a combination of proteins and carbohydrates) and fish for the first stage of her regime, Dukan allows more variety--his definition of protein for the first \\"Attack\\" phase of his plan consists of lean meats which include beef and organ meat, poultry, fish, shellfish, ham &lt;wow>, eggs, and non-fat dairy.  Fattier meats like pork are included only in their leanest forms.<br /><br />Whereas, Klauer's Phase 1 continues until the dieter is within five pounds of their goal weight, the duration of Dukan's Attack phase can run from as little as one day to ten days depending upon how much weight one must lose, and how many failed diets the dieter participated in during their dieting history.  Once the initial `Attack' jumpstart is completed, meaning only protein as defined by Dukan and a limited amount of oat bran is consumed, the dieter graduates to the \\"Cruise\\" step--where unlimited green, not starchy, vegetables are added to the mix.  It is in this phase, that the dieter continues, alternating a day of pure protein with a day of protein plus greens, until goal weight is achieved.<br /><br />Klauer's second phase is similar to Dukan's \\"Consolidation\\" step where a health-promoting eating style is continued with the reintroduction of foods that were restricted in the first phases.  Klauer allows chicken and beef to be added in the first week of her maintenance preparation phase.  As the weeks go on, she includes fruits, grains, nuts etc, gradually allowing the body to readapt to what it missed and eventually leveling off at goal weight.  Dukan explains that the length of this phase is dependent on the amount of weight lost by the dieter.  For example, if twenty pounds were lost, this phase will last 100 days--five days for every pound lost.  Dukan strongly feels that the body's first inclination after losing fat storages will be to replenish them.  Therefore, it is of paramount importance to ease back into an everyday eating plan without giving the body the opportunity to rebound.  Dukan allows starchy foods, fruits (no bananas, raisins or cherries), cheese, whole grain bread and lean lamb and pork roasts at this point, but strictly enforces that in each week one day is still set aside for pure protein only.  He claims that most people disregard this phase of dieting, and unfortunately regain most, if not more, of the weight they strove so hard to lose.  Two celebratory `eat whatever you want' meals are also allowed in this phase.<br /><br />Dukan's last phase is \\"Stabilization\\" which I interpret as `mindful eating.'  Being aware of what you eat is as important as the gasoline that racecar pilots use in their high-priced vehicles for optimal performance.  If you want your body to run at peak perfection, give it the best fuel--chips and French fries are delicious, but don't give you the mileage that you need to live fully within your 24 hour per day time slot.<br /><br />For this final phase that lasts for the remainder of your life, Dukan recommends keeping the ideas of his \\"Consolidation\\" in mind while making food choices that will include each food group.  For example, when eating eggs consider egg whites instead of the whole egg, non-fat dairy instead of whole and fish instead of beef.  He insists that one day a week continue to be a \\"protein only\\" day.  Oat bran consumption is upped to three tablespoons per day.  And, of course, flushing the body with water and keeping it well oiled with exercise is the no-brainer approach to a good life.<br /><br />Dukan's book devotes some time to the discussion of women's hormonal changes and how these affect weight and the effectiveness of diet.  Using myself as the only benchmark for my remarks, I find that following the dieting premises of Jana Klauer, Ann Louise Gittleman ([[ASIN:0071383832 The Fat Flush Plan]]) or Pierre Dukan will result in success no matter what your age.  Vigilance is key--selecting good food that benefits your body is paramount.  Unfortunately eating junk--food that your body really doesn't need to function--will result in your looking like you eat junk.  And that ultimately provides the bottom line:  You are what you eat--if you wish to indulge--by all means, do so.  However, be aware that you will look as if you do.<br /><br />In addition, as you wean yourself off unnecessary foods, you will find that you don't need as much food to accomplish what you wish.  Junk is addictive; it makes you want more.  Portion control with real food will naturally occur as your program of `mindful' eating progresses.<br /><br />Personally, I find Klauer and Gittleman's plans more doable; they, however, are portion controlled which may be more difficult for those who are used to eating what they want, whenever.  I say this, because, pure protein even for a limited amount of time for some may disturb the digestive system--surely the oat bran helps, but some salads and green vegetables make such moves much smoother.  If you decide to go the Dukan route, check out Teavana's Dieter's Tea--a blend of caffeinated teas that will aid with this problem.  Drink before 2 PM as the caffeine may keep you from having a good night's sleep.<br /><br />In the past, I have recommended raw food plans that push a variety of green juices and a large consumption of fruits and vegetables.  Without the addition some protein, these diets weakened me.  My digestive track did work well, but I was unable to work out in the way that I like without feeling sore.  The additional diary and protein help me immensely.<br /><br />Bottom line?  \\"The Dukan Diet\\" is definitely a step above the fad diets that are advertised nightly on infomercials and hawked relentlessly on the Internet. With thirty years experience, French doctor, Pierre Dukan tells the dieter what works in simple language that is easy to understand.  For those who like \\"[[ASIN:0375710515 French Women Don't Get Fat]],\\" look at Dukan's plan as its prequel--the step that will get you ready for Guiliano's plan.  Those already conditioned for portion control try Jana Klauer's \\"[[ASIN:B002KE482U How the Rich Get Thin: Park Avenue's Top Diet Doctor Reveals the Secrets to Losing Weight and Feeling Great]].\\"  For those who want to add supplements and rich flaxseed oil, look at Ann Louise Gittleman's \\"[[ASIN:0071383832 The Fat Flush Plan]].\\"  All these diets advocate `mindful eating' through food education and look at the body as a machine with necessary requirements.<br />Recommended.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"of `mindful' eating progresses. B0008ENIAC|4.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />For those who believe that films must remain 'faithful' to their source material should take a look at the original 1945 film version of 'Mildred Pierce'; it's an example of the 'right' way to adapt a novel into a screenplay. Contrast it with the turgid, long-winded 2011 HBO version of 'Pierce'. There's actually no comparison: the 1945 version is wholly entertaining while the recent adaptation will bore you to tears.<br /><br />Mildred Pierce was the perfect vehicle for Joan Crawford. Ironically here she plays a mother whose love is pretty much unconditional toward her bad seed daughter Veda (in real life, however, Crawford used 'tough love' on her own children with disastrous results). Unlike the novel (and the recent HBO Version), the noirish murder scene constitutes the inciting incident and linchpin of the entire plot.<br /><br />The break into the Second Act begins during the police interrogation as Mildred recounts her break up with husband Bert and quest for financial independence. When a waitress is fired during an argument with another waitress over tips, Mildred just happens to be in the right place at the right time and is hired on the spot. It's these neat little scenes that add to the overall verisimilitude of the plot.<br /><br />Act two introduces us to Mildred's conflict with Wally, Bert's former real estate partner and daughter Veda. As for Wally--he's distinctively played by Jack Carson as the seedy salesman who can't resist trying to putting his paws all over Mildred (he's a serial groper!). Ann Blyth is brilliant, making the most of the chief supporting players' part, as the evil Veda. The character is so over the top that one can scarcely believe there are people like this in real life. Veda can't control her contempt for her mother and yes teenagers often do rebel against parental authority; but the disdain is usually a lot more subtle than what we see here. Still, Veda's exaggerated (and escalating) misbehavior is what makes the film so enjoyable (there is a catharsis of sorts as the audience gets to 'hiss the villain').<br /><br />If there is one sentimental sequence 'Mildred Pierce', it's when younger daughter Ray contracts pneumonia and dies. The scene is a bit drawn out but provides an explanation for Mildred's continuing attachment to Veda--the loss of Ray causes Mildred to try and protect the only child she has left!<br /><br />The stakes get higher in Act 2. Monte is temporarily thrown 'under the bus' when he refuses Mildred's request not to indulge Veda and stay away from her. Then Veda goes ahead and cons her new husband and his mother out of $10,000 by pretending that she's pregnant. There's that great iconic scene where Veda ends up slapping Mildred and she throws her out of the house. Meanwhile, Mildred finds success in the restaurant business by opening up a chain of restaurants. Should I mention that Mildred's former supervisor Ida (deliciously played by Eve Arden) is the comic relief with her sassy comments about men. Leave it to kind-hearted Bert (who finally has gotten his act together), to bring Veda and Mildred back together. This happens when he brings Mildred to a seedy nightclub where Veda now works as a low rent singer.<br /><br />The dark moment of Act 2 occurs after Mildred agrees to marry Monte and give him one third of the business (she does this to perhaps improve her social standing in Veda's eyes). Monte betrays Mildred by selling his share of the business along with Wally, which bankrupts her. The ultimate indignity of course is that Monte begins an affair with Veda.<br /><br />What makes Mildred Pierce so engaging is that not only is Veda damaged goods but so is Mildred. She's willing to send an innocent man to jail to save her daughter when she tries to set up Wally as the killer.<br /><br />Note that the story is not without a few false notes. How can the police arrest Bert at the beginning of the film? Yes, they have his gun and no denial that he was responsible; but they don't actually have a confession, so the evidence seems rather flimsy. Then there's the ending itself--the murder feels a bit tacked on as if the screenwriter couldn't come up with something more creative and needed to rush things to an almost immediate conclusion. It's really not much of a twist ending as Monte's murder seems rather predictable.<br /><br />Despite the minor pitfalls, 'Mildred Pierce' remains a highly entertaining part noir, part soap opera, that manages to thrill audiences even to this day.ave a confession, so the evidence seems rather flimsy. Then there's the ending itself--the murder feels a bit tacked on as if the screenwriter couldn't come up with something more creative and needed to rush things to an almost immediate conclusion. It's really not much of a twist ending as Monte's murder seems rather predictable.    Despite the minor pitfalls, 'Mildred Pierce' remains a highly entertaining part noir, part soap opera, that manages to thrill audiences even to this day.

B0015HOFZI|5.0|The technical specifications say that this turntable is not Mac compatible, and while it is true that the software that ships with it is not Mac-compatible, I was able to hook up this turntable to my Mac Pro running 10.6.<br /><br />To listen to or record audio, you can go to Sound under System Preferences, click the Input tab, and select USB Audio Codec (after plugging in the turntable to a USB port on your Mac).  Then to hear or record music, launch GarageBand, set the Input Source to USB Audio Codec, turn on the Monitor, and turn off feedback protection.  From there you can easily record to a track in GarageBand or simply listen to the music.<br /><br />Other people suggest that you need to download a program called Audacity, but this is not necessary, GarageBand works just fine.<br /><br />Update: You can also use a fantastic app called AirFoil for Mac to not only listen to the turntable on your Mac (instead of GarageBand, as I noted above), but also to send the audio to other Macs, AppleTVs, iPhones, iPads, and iPod Touches.  Using this app I can now listen to vinyl in any room in my house.<br /><br />2nd Update: In addition to the preceding options, Rogue Amoeba makes a great free app called Line In, this free app is available from [...] .  Using Line-In, just launch the App and select the USB audio codec as the source and the audio will play through to your Mac's speakers.

0525422943|5.0|Beautiful. Absolutely beautiful. I've read books about love and books about heartbreak and sacrifice, but none of them have amounted to the literary beauty of Where She Went. Books don't need to be action-packed or filled with complex plots to be considered a \\"great read.\\" Instead, they can have two characters, both with deeply instigating personalities and a passion for opposite types of music. Throughout my teenage life, I've acquired a taste for both rock and classical, and it makes my heart swell with enchantment when I see the two styles resonating to create a cacophony of emotions.     Adam and Mia were an unlikely high school couple who've now gone their separate ways in life. Mia's accident brought them close, but then it pushed them apart. Adam, whose band has hurled into the depths fame, is living the life of a rockstar. Mia's life couldn't have been more different. She's a child prodigy in the world of classical music whose career is only beginning. One last fateful night in New York City leaves them both reminiscing about the past, enduring the present, and fighting their way into an uncertain future. Adam promised Mia he'd doing anything for her if only she'd stay. But for Mia, leaving would have been so much easier than staying.    Usually, I'm no fan of romances involving careless and shallow teenagers in love. But Adam and Mia are different. Despite their youth, their love was the kind that reached into the soul and bonded together for eternity. All the suffering they've endured was the kind of passion and pain that fueled their lives emotionally. Their music, their life, and their love was in completely harmony. And that in itself presented a beautiful and complicated obstacle for two polar opposites meant to attract. There's something about Gayle Forman's writing that's hauntingly elegant. In the midst of her story, I'd sometimes find myself stopping just to relish the poetry-like descriptions, or to soak in the radiant flood of overwhelming emotions. I found myself completely engulfed in the wave of grief and hope that was the foundation of Adam and Mia's relationship. Towards the last few chapters, I nearly felt my heart shattering at Mia's proposition of closure. For a moment, their friendship seemed to hang in a delicate balance. One look could send them down opposite sides of a bridge, while it could also send them crashing into one another's arms. The ending, however, was a song that still echoes in the back of my mind. I have never read a book more poetic, pure, and musical. It was stunning. It was painful. It was beautiful. It was the strings of an electric guitar and a cello playing in harmony.     -Carina L. B000000YG5|4.0|After repeatedly seeing this album mentioned as Rollins' best as well as a jazz masterpiece, I ordered it and listened to it several times with pleasure. After repeated listenings, however, I've begun to question the basis for this seemingly universal high praise.<br /><br />Certainly, more than a few jazz critics rate the album very highly. For example, Joe Goldberg, in his [[ASIN:B003477HA2 Jazz Masters of the Fifties]], states: \\"This LP, recorded with Roach, bassist Doug Watkins, and pianist Tommy Flanagan, is notable for two tracks, Moritat and Blue 7. With the latter piece, serious criticism of Rollins' work began. In the first issue of the highly influential Jazz Review, Gunther Schuller called Blue 7 'an example of a real variation technique. The improvisation is based not only on a harmonic sequence but on a melodic idea as well... what Sonny Rollins has added conclusively to the scope of jazz improvisation is the idea of developing and varying a main theme, and not just a secondary motive or phrase which the player happens to hit upon in the course of his improvisation and which in itself is unrelated to the 'head' of the composition.' \\"<br /><br />As for Moritat, Goldberg has nothing in particular to say. Personally, though, Mac the Knife is not one of my favorite older tunes and Rollins' improvisations do not make the standard any more pleasurable for me. Additionally, Rollins' bouncy, Carribbean-tinged tune, St Thomas, while light-hearted and with an infectious beat, wears rather thin upon repeated listenings.<br /><br />The masterwork on the album is indeed Blue 7, which I like a lot, but had jazz historian/composer Gunther Schuller not written his influential article, I suspect that it would remain among the many warmly appreciated but largely unheralded jazz compositions from the 1950s.<br /><br />Despite my personal idiosyncracies, still a highly recommended CD for fans of Sonny Rollins and fans of 1950s jazz.

B003UESJHE|5.0|Rarely does film-making come in as solid a package as it does in Tom Hooper's THE KING'S SPEECH.  You have top-notch directing; perfect casting; an engaging script; beautiful cinematography; and a story with a lot of room for emotional interpretation.  It concerns Bertie--soon to be King George VI--and his stammer, and the Australian linguist hired to cure him.<br /><br />What's so surprising about SPEECH is that there's little surprise here; everything is just as you would expect it to be (except for the cursing scenes, which come as a pleasant change of pace).  And yet, the film comes off smoothly, from the stellar lead performances (another fine turn from Colin Firth, and Geoffrey Rush at his best) to the minor roles (a superb Helena Bohnam Carter as the future queen, an under-used Guy Pearce as Bertie's brother, and Michael Gambon as their father).  This is a near-flawless film, which is perhaps its only flaw; it could stand to be a little grittier, a little less predictable.  But it's hard to complain when a film hits all the right emotional buttons.  It's too soon to tell THE KING'S SPEECH will be a classic, but it will definitely go down as a fine piece of cinematic integrity.

0060853972|2.0|I don't know how it's possible that I didn't love this book.<br /><br />The promotional blurbs on and inside the cover compare it to Douglas Adams and Monty Python, and I'm a great fan of absurdist British wit.  I've always been preoccupied with questions of ontology, theodicy, and ethics, and I have a decidedly irreverent sense of humor, so an offbeat novel about the Apocalypse should have been right up my alley.  And how could I NOT love a novel featuring a character named Anathema Device?<br /><br />I've read Neil Gaiman before, and I can't say he's one of my favorite authors.  \\"American Gods\\" was based around a truly original premise, and contained passages of sheer brilliance that I've never been able to forget - and yet it was a novel I can't really imagine wanting to read again.  \\"Coraline\\" left me cold, and although I loved the beginning of \\"The Graveyard Book,\\" I wasn't terribly thrilled about the ending.  I'd never read Terry Pratchett, but a friend of mine whose opinion I trust told me that, although he often has something wise to say, his delivery tends to be over-the-top.  I should have listened.<br /><br />There are some moments of absolute genius in \\"Good Omens.\\"  The quirky philosophy the authors explore is delightful, and similar to some of my own private reflections, though at times it ventures out of the realm of moral ambiguity and into the murky waters of moral nihilism.  There are some passages so unexpected and true that you'll want to grab the next available person and read them out loud: \\"God does not play dice with the universe; He plays an ineffable game of His own devising, which might be compared, from the perspective of any of the other players, to being involved in an obscure and complex version of poker in a pitch-dark room, with blank cards, for infinite stakes, with a Dealer who won't tell you the rules, and who *smiles all the time*.\\"<br /><br />It's hard to imagine that a novel with so much going for it could prove disappointing, but I found myself slogging through the pages as much to get it over with as from any genuine interest.  There were enough witty and interesting moments to keep me from giving up altogether, and it was certainly a fast read.  However, the convoluted plot felt forced as often as it felt genuinely clever.  Perhaps, seeing as a major theme of the novel is the battle of good vs. evil as a sort of morally empty cosmic chess game, this feeling of contrivance was intentional, but if so, it didn't work for me.  It didn't help that the humor was so juvenile, absurdity for absurdity's sake (interspersed with moments of genuine wittiness): I have no doubt I would have laughed out loud at every page of this novel twenty years ago, but now it simply feels excessive and silly.<br /><br />Reading \\"Good Omens,\\" I kept thinking of \\"Galapagos,\\" by Kurt Vonnegut.  The two novels have a lot in common: a diverse cast of characters brought together by an unusual series of events, a truly omniscient narrator whose witty asides help move the plot along and hint at things to come, an irreverent sense of humor applied to events on a global scale.  \\"Galapagos\\" worked for me; \\"Good Omens\\" didn't.  If you're in the mood for some intelligent absurdity, I suggest you pick up \\"Galapagos\\" instead.ugh the pages as much to get it over with as from any genuine interest. It's difficult to even justify calling it 288 pages long, as the margins and line spacing are larger than I've seen in most books and it also includes several pictures and entire script excerpts from 30 Rock and SNL.  I'd say it's realistically about half the advertised length, though it's a totally breezy read.  I managed to finish it in one weekend, and I'm generally a pretty slow reader.  It's easy to pickup and difficult to put down, though it's a book I could really only recommend for hardcore Tina Fey fans.

It is the tale of his encounters with these men and his skepticism of all religions.<br />  The story starts with Mitch Albom's childhood Rabbi asking him the favor of writing and delivering his eulogy when he passes after service one day.  He is caught off guard by rabbi's request because he no longer lives in the area and is not a regular participant at services.  Finally he agrees to the request with the condition that he gets to know him better as a man.  Reb agrees to his condition and this makes Mitch a bit uncomfortable because he know it will put him  back into his synagogue and faith he left behind years ago.<br />  He begins his weekly trips back and forth from Detroit to visit the Reb, with many health scares over the years and learns the life story of Reb and the strength of his faith thru many of life's trials.  He nick names him the `singing rabbi' because of his love for song.  Mitch begins to develop a relationship with Reb that becomes unexpected to him and it opens his eyes to his upbringing and the faith he never understand.<br />  Closer to home in Detroit, Albom is looking for an organization to support and encounters Henry a Christian Pastor, a reformed drug dealer and convict.  Henry, the leader for \\"I Am My Brother's Keeper Ministries\\" preaches to the poor, cares for the homeless, criminals, addicts and anyone who needs him, for free because his church comes first.  With any religion Albom is skeptic, but he is very skeptical about Henry's motives and his faith.  After several meetings Albom finally decides to visit Henry's church without telling him and he discovers that even with a hole in the decaying church's roof Henry stays positive and faithful that God with take care of it when the time is right.  Obstacles will not stop him for caring for the needy and doing the job he promised God he would do in His name.<br />  As he goes between the worlds of these two men he knows how different each is yet he sees how similar they are in their survival, faith and trust in God.  Reb embraces his retirement, health issues and his own death as it approaches and Pastor Henry trusts that his mission for the needy will be met and his church will remain open.  He doesn't understand it and is suspicious at times but as the years go on he sees how their faith transforms their lives.<br />  The ending is exactly what is expected, Mitch Albom giving Reb's eulogy, but what isn't expected is how the lives of these two different men change how Mitch Albom sees his own upbringing, his faith and the trust people place in God.  It transformed him and fulfilled a part of his life that was empty.<br />  This is an easy read book and not a religious book by any means.  It is the true, honest story of Mitch Albom's journey of regaining his faith and losing his cynical outlook on religion.  It is the touching story of an ex-convict who changed his ways to help others and the story of a rabbi who enjoyed his life to the fullest to the very end.  It is a book of experiences.<br />  Mitch Albom did a wonderful job with this story and I highly recommend it.  Once you start reading you won't put it down and it will make you think once you begin to close the cover on the final page.discovers that even with a hole in the decaying church's roof Henry stays positive and faithful that God with take care of it when the time is right. B004QDCYJC|5.0|Ludicrous, overblown, pompous and powerful are all adjectives that can be applied to successful power metal if it's done well and here Symfonia tick all the boxes with a release that is as colourful, melodic and frenetic as you would expect from members of Stratovarius, Angra, Helloween and Sonata Arctica but there's one word yet missing from the list, and that word is FUN. Like the finale of a particularly catchy musical number, the canvas that Symfonia work from is huge and every song explodes like so many coloured glitter bombs whilst the music is both powerful and distressingly catchy, sticking in your mind whether you want it to or not proving that the gaudily coloured and stylised artwork is the perfect representation of the content of `In Paradisium'.     Eschewing anything as subtle as an introduction, Symfonia rock from the very first moment of the album when guitarist Timo Tolkki unleashes a riff that is pure old school metal backed by Uli Kusch's fast-paced drums and Mikko Harkin's almost Baroque keyboard attack. While musically invigorating, however, `Fields of Avalon' belongs to singer Andre Matos (of Angra fame) who tests his vocal range to the very limit with a brave, impassioned and brilliant performance that will delight fans of his own band and of heavy power metal in general. It's all completely bonkers of course, with the band unable to resist massed vocals on the chorus and a hook line so brazenly catchy it could have been pulled from some Broadway-hugging musical. Yet what keeps you hooked is that all important sense of fun. Like Warmen or Sonata Arctica at their best you can feel through every steroid-enhanced riff and earthquake-causing beat that the band are having the time of their lives blasting the song out and its impossible not to get swept up in it no matter how hard you may try to resist. Second track `come by the hills' has a similar, epic feel with hints of both Iron Maiden and Nightwish bursting through the opening riff and Andre'sBruce Dickinson-esque vocals. It's less overtly theatrical than its forebear although the chorus is once again more addictive than crack. Getting progressively heavier as things move on, `Santiago' is an absolute belter with a huge central guitar riff and another stunning performance from Andre who is capable of multiple styles from gritty hard rock to soaring high notes, often in the same song and who proves to be the perfect front man for this ambitious, energetic band. Certainly `Santiago' has bags of style and the stunning guitar/keyboard break at its core allows both Timo and Mikko to show off their dexterity while bassist Jari Kainulainen more than keeps up in the dash to the finale.     Shifting pace, the initial thought at the start of `Alayna' is that the band have covered a Duran Duran song, before the delicately picked guitar shifts the focus more in the direction of Sonata Arctica's `San Sebastian', which is no bad place to be at all. Emotive and giving Andre a chance to shine without the band's usual `kitchen-sink' approach in the background, it's a gorgeous power ballad that would, if there was any justice in the world, scale the charts destroying hip-hop and so called R&B numbers by the score before arriving proudly at the number one spot. That outcome, alas, seems a touch unlikely so we'll settle just for knowing that it's a great track. Seemingly having had enough down time, the band come scorching back in with the brutal `forevermore', the opening riff of which actually recalls System of a down's `BYOBB' before things take a more obvious power metal direction with the introduction of the vocals. Equally victorious is the chugging, folk-metal stylings of `Pilgrim road' which sees Timo shine brightly once again with a stunning and fluid guitar solo that stands out as this particular song's finest moment. Opting for a darker, more operatic metal feel the title track sees massed choral vocals and synth strings vying for space against a mid-tempo riffbefore everything falls away to reveal Andre's naked voice and a gentle acoustic guitar to great effect highlighting the fact that Symfonia don't always follow a predictable route preferring to keep the listener guessing what is going to come next rather than pandering to expectations. Hence when the song moves back through its opening riff and then charges off on an Iron Maiden gallop you perhaps shouldn't be too surprised and the track is another highlight that rocks and delights in equal measure with Andre turning in a performance that recalls Geoff Tate at his peak.     The final part of the album kicks off with the riff-heavy `Rhapsody in black' which is pure old school metal with a hint of `Empire'-era Queensryche amidst its driving riffs and vaguely pop-styled verses. `I walk in neon', meanwhile, is aptly titled with the band turning in a blistering performance that begins with a simple keyboard riff and then turns into the sort of chugging riff guaranteed to get the denim and leather-clad hordes into a froth while the chorus is huge beyond belief. The final track, `don't let me go' sees the band in a reflective mood with a cello added to the mix and a gentle acoustic strum opening a track that will undoubtedly prove to be a sing-a-long par excellence in the live environment with Andre stating \\"don't let me go, I'm here to stay, enjoy the moment, live day by day\\" which could well be the band's official motto such is the vibrancy of their output.     Overall you probably have some idea if this is for you or not. I know many metal fans who run a mile at power metal and many more who embrace it wholeheartedly suggesting that it is one of the many `marmite' sub genres of metal. However, if you do like strong, driving, melodic and passionate metal then you'll go a bundle on this colourful and interesting release. To be sure there are times when the over-the-top approach gets a bit much and you yearn for a simple, sledgehammer riff to churn through the various elements but overall this is a great example of talented musicians playing the music they love and it's almost impossible to not get a kick out of such dedicated artistry. To sum up, then, `In Paradisum' is a vital, vibrant, fun album that will thrill power metal fans and classic rock fans alike.

0684800713|3.0|The book is considered great--a description of the lost generation after World War I, and an exemplar of Hemingway's unusual, laconic prose, what Malcom Cowley called \\"The Middle American Prose Style.\\" I've read the book a number of times over the years, and never quite enjoy it. The characters seem juvenile, all of one type, all variations of Hemingway's antiquated notion of manhood. The female protagonist, Lady Brett, is just a male fantasy, the femme fatale no man can have, who is unhappy and unfullfilled herself. These vamps seem to populate the literature of the twenties--modeled on famous bohemian heiresses or actresses of the day. Jake, the main character, is another Hemingway self-description, alienated and in pain, with little wit or cleverness in self-reflection.  With uninteresting characters, a somewhat primitive ethos of manhood, prose style that may have been novel in its inception in the twenties but flat upon reading now, and not much in the way of unusual narrative, I am puzzled this book retains its high acclaim. One wonder if it is simply because, with its short words, it is easy to read. And oddly, nevertheless I continue to read and re-read it every once in a while--mostly because of the unwaveringly dry prose--the same kind of stoic beauty as a midwestern plain. Damon LaBarbera, PhD

B002JIEYL2|4.0|This is not one of Black Sabbath's best known albums. Their first three have that distinction. The only song from this album that became a bonafide hit was the title track, which became a classic rock staple. The whole album is fantastic though. Here they refine their sound and add synth, orchestral and keyboard textures. Even the novelty track \\"Fluff\\", which is a fan-hated softer side of the metal legends is a well-executed and composed song. Maturity is the word used by most critics reviewing this album. Although their earlier stuff had a bit more instant appeal, this album doesn't take long to get used to and after it takes hold it doesn't let go.     Lyrically, it is less gothic than earlier albums, with Sabbath concentrating on modern (for the time) themes more. Sabbra Cadabra, which is a paean to pagan witches, comes off as one of the strangest love songs ever written. Musically, the synths, pianos, flutes and other accents enhance Sabbath's style. Any fan of heavy metal, classic rock or dreary music will love this incarnation of the masters of the \\"doom blues\\". I bought the Japanese edition and compared it to the Sanctuary Deluxe remasters and I think that this has a clearer, more defined sound. The Sanctuary editions are excellent but tend to try to squeeze more volume out of the remaster by sacrificing dynamic range and it results in slight distortion at higher volume.     The only true classic Sabbath tunes on this album are:  Sabbath Bloody Sabbath  Sabbra Cadabra  Killing Yourself to Live    My favorite Sabbath albums in order from best to worst are:    PARANOID  BLACK SABBATH  MASTER OF REALITY  SABBATH BLOODY SABBATH  Vol.4  HEAVEN AND HELL  THE MOB RULES    PARANOID through MASTER all get 5 stars, with the others getting 4. All strongly recommended.

031237593X|3.0|Sharon Penman tells a great yarn.  She also puts in the hours of research necessary to give historical narrative an authentic ring.  However, with The Sunne In Splendour the author takes a possibility and tries to stretch it into a probability, which just isn't happening.  I have no doubt that the Tudor propaganda mill had a good time grinding Diccon into dust.  However just as someone may be out to get a paranoid person, the vilified may indeed be villainous.<br /><br />Penman fingers the Duke of Buckingham for the crime of murdering the little princes. Hilariously, while decrying the vilification of Richard, she has no trouble smearing Buckingham with a broad brush.  Of course there is absolutely no extant evidence to support her claim, but so what?  It makes for a good read.  The problem  for Penman is of the three suspects (Henry Tudor, Richard and Buckingham) it is only Richard who has motive, means AND opportunity.<br /><br />Furthermore, to Richard's 15th Century mind, there is a \\"justice\\" in disposing of the little princes.  The Rivers-Grey family are upstarts crowding out the old nobility.  For a Plantagenat to have to endure a Rivers' being elevated to his equal stature would be an affront.  To see a low-birth family who had supported the Lancasters until Elizabeth caught Edward's eye would be insufferable.  And once the rumors start flying Edward had been betrothed before (and therefore his issue with Elizabeth illegitimate) well, NOBODY in the world had more incentive to kill those kids-- family pride or personal position was alone reason enough.  The two together was just irresistible.<br /><br />Of Buckingham, Henry Tudor or Richard, all three would definitely have like to see the boys dead.  All three could easily pay assassins to do their dirty work.  Richard, however, as Lord Protector is the only one with total access to the Tower of London.  Remembering the Tower of London isn't just a Tower, but the 15th century's version of a maximum security prison, this is an essential consideration Penman ignores. Two Royal Princes just vanish from the Tower of London.  Poof!  And nobody thinks to tell the Lord Protector til so much time has passed it would look suspicious if he sounds an alarum.  Really?!?!<br /><br />Richard, the brilliant military commander and leader somehow forgot that security for the princes was important?  He forgot to use his best people and staffed the Tower with venal traitors who could be bought off to murder children?  Richard's just  a victim of peoples' high expectations--i.e., expecting the Lord Protector to protect his charges? Outrageous!!! What starts as  an interesting premise just collapses beneath it's own weighty contradictions.<br /><br />The truth is anyone of the three suspects would have happily killed the boys, as they happily killed each other when circumstances allowed.  All three probably lusted for power in equal measure.  However it was Richard with unlimited means and access to the princes.  He counted on people being afraid of the unrest that accompanies minor kings and he miscalculated people's revulsion.  Remember, after Tewksbury, the Lancasters were so effectively beaten there was no real opposition to the triumphant Yorkists.  And it wasn't that bad a gamble--after so much bloodshed what were two more deaths?  He miscalculated the enormous difference between honorable death on a battlefield and slitting the throats of children in their beds.  Three years later when Richard has overreached, his enemies have to go to the Welsh branch of the Lancaster family, the Tudors, to rally around a candidate.  But they do.<br /><br />It speaks volumes that after a generation of civil war, a nation so depleted and bleeding would be willing to go yet one more round. Half the country would  prefer to crown a Welsh earl (an upstart if there ever was one  before kneeling to a bona fide Plantagenat prince.) Nobody is THAT misunderstood.  Richard must have been truly despised.  To paraphrase Oscar Wilde:  \\"To lose a nephew is a great tragedy.  To lose two seems careless.\\"<br /><br />Diccon done it.  And he paid.gedy.  To lose two seems careless.\\"    Diccon done it.  And he paid.

B004G6009K|4.0|As someone who grew up watching Disney (and other animated) movies and appreciates the art form as much as live action films for its freedom and creative license, I'm often one of the few childless adults in the theater when new animated films come out. There have been a rash of really good animated movies lately, and I was excited about this one. I think, for kids, this movie hits the mark very well; for adults, it's good but not quite classic material.<br /><br />The story has all the potential to be a classic, and the animation - while not the quite the same style as the hand-drawn classics - has its own charm that makes up for the occasional plasticy look. Mother Gothel (the villain) had the potential to be a villain on par with Frollo (Hunchback of Notre Dame) himself - a weaselly, malicious, evil user who plays mind games to keep their charge in line. And the music was beautiful, with good to great lyrics (depending on the song). So why on earth did they choose a pop singer for their music, or turn Mother Gothel's potential complex and brilliant character into a snide, obvious, one-dimensional meanie?<br /><br />The great music from the Disney classics is great because it is sung by people with the proper vocal range and steady voices; perhaps most importantly of all, they are singing to make beautiful music, not because of their trademark genre. From Hunchback of Notre Dame to Aladdin, Mulan to Beauty and the Beast, Pocahontas to Sleeping Beauty, these songs are memorable for their classic beauty. No offense to Mandy Moore - who is a fine pop singer - but her singing was not at all suited for this role, and strongly detracted from the film.<br /><br />As for Mother Gothel's character, her constant snide-jabs-followed-by-quick-apologies had the effect of weakening the character greatly. I know people like this - who insult you and then laugh it off with, \\"Sorry, just kidding.\\" Other than themselves, I don't know anyone who thinks they are clever or witty; they are clearly, obviously nasty, and not even terribly intelligent in going about voicing their malice. Gothel, however, is not supposed to be an annoying, mean dimwit; she's supposed to be very clever and manipulative. Had they had her insult without offering apologies, or insult without laughing it off - and, when Rapunzel was insulted, chided her for being too touchy or sensitive, the effect would have been much better. She would not only have been mean and hurt her daughter's feelings, but she would have then laid a guilt trip on her as well for being \\"too sensitive\\". (I've known people like this, too, and they are a heck of a lot more effective than the other type). Disney * tried * to do this, but failed - instead, Mother Gothel comes across as a mean (but obviously so), catty, snide witch, rather than a clever manipulator. Instead of a female Frollo personality, they got a mosquito.<br /><br />These were my two biggest gripes with this movie, and what, to me, kept it from 5-star-classic material. Now, that said, this is still a good film - and one of the best ones Disney has put out in years, literally. Younger kids will probably not notice either of these points, and older ones may or may not find them as significant as I did. 0312370849|2.0|During the Vlodrome d'Hiver round-up in 1942, French police tore thousands of French jews, mostly women and children, from their homes and kept them in squalid conditions in a stadium before transporting them on French owned trains to their deaths at Auschwitz.  This incident is so blindingly tragic that I would think even an inferior author couldn't help but tell the story in a way that is heartbreaking, infuriating, and fully alive. I was wrong. de Rosnay tells the story in a split narrative, starting with a French child, Sarah, who is rounded up with her parents in the Vl d'Hiver, torn from them forcibly, and sent to a camp where thousands of children are kept in a camp without food, water or adult supervision. Before the family is taken from home, Sarah hides her 3 year old brother in a hidden cupboard that she alone carries the key to. Her story tells her brave attempt to escape from the camp and rescue him. The narrative then switches to Julia Jarmond, a bland, self-pitying American ex-pat living in Paris. Compared to the hellish events of 1942, Julia's story never had a chance to be compelling. As it is, it is painfully banal.     SPOILERS    From the first descriptions of her charming, rogueish French husband, I could tell the author was setting Julia up leave him. He teases her. They don't listen to each other. And on and on. The issue that finally makes them reach the point of no return is outrageously contrived- I didn't believe for an instant any people would act or react this way. How on earth did deRosnay think this lame story of upper middle class marital ennui would tie in satisfactorily with Jewish genocide, aided by the French?       While writing a story for a magazine, Julia discovers that her apartment was once inhabited by Sarah's family, and she goes on a search to find out about Sarah, and eventually find her. Along the way, she discovers secrets that have been hidden by her in-laws for 60 years. This is the key reason why this story never gets off the ground- there is simply no urgency or narrative drive in discovering what happened 60 years after the fact. Of course it was terrible. Of course Sarah's life was shattered. Not only that, but Julia's family secrets are contrived for no apparent reason other than moving this weak plot along. The events of the Vl d'Hiver and Sarah have been kept secret from Julia's in-laws for 60 years because of an inexplicable death-bed promise from the family patriarch. Why? The events were well known. France made holocaust denial a crime. There was no reason everyone would have kept it quiet. Julia's obsession with finding out about Sarah is also implausible. (So are the obstacles she faces- her husband keeps protesting with arguments like \\"leave the past alone.\\" Pathetic.) She didn't know about her existence only months ago, but now Julia is jetting off back and forth from the Atlantic and showing up at doors without thinking to call and verify her information first. And why? What is her burning goal? To say that she's sorry. That she remembers. None of this adds up to a story that keeps you turning pages. Character development is non-existant. We know nothing of Sarah after she is about 11. We hear nothing from people who actually survived or witnessed the Vlodrome d'Hiver. The only point the author makes, which she makes over and over, with little variation, is that it was terrible, and people should remember. She sheds no light, adds no insight, fails to humanize. This story should have been told from the perspective of people that were actually involved. Its hard to believe that I read a novel about one of the greatest human tragedies of all history, and the only thing that was really dealt with was Julia's pathetic relationship troubles and middling sense of self.     If you want to read a book about WWII that adds something new, something humanizing and heartbreaking and worthwhile, try the Book Thief or the Guernsey Potato Peel Society.
