1594202842|4.0|Amy Chua has ignited an important debate regarding the effects of parental expectations on children's intellectual accomplishments. She makes clear throughout her book that she values success in academics and achievement in classical music over the more mainstream American obsession with social acceptance and popularity. Displaying irony, wit, and humor, Chua admits on nearly every page that she was overzealous at times in her pursuit of excellence for her children. While some readers may view her disapproval of play dates and sleepovers as neurotically strict, I find it understandable since this culture often allows socially dominant children to gain too much power over their peers. Children who have learned self-discipline and intellectual focus may become immune to the influence of the louche, lazy, or obnoxious kids prevalent in many middle and high schools. We have all had enough of the witchy queen bee, the slothful pothead, and the brutal wolfish bully. Whether readers agree or disagree with Chua, her memoir incites them to examine the ways in which parental authority shapes children's values.    While Chua realizes the flaws in her parenting method, she demonstrates far less awareness of the status anxiety and social snobbery that plague many of her attitudes and actions. As a tenured professor at Yale who has written two well-regarded books in her field, she belongs to a cultural elite whose economic and social privileges far exceed those of most Americans. Since she knows these privileges are hard to gain and maintain, she is determined with a vengeance to keep them. One of the ways that she secures her upper class status is by enabling her children to succeed in high cultural pursuits that may be out of reach for those from less privileged backgrounds. In Chua's world, composers such as Bartok, Kodaly, and Prokofiev are status markers as powerful as Yale, Harvard, and Princeton. On the one hand, Chua's love for classical music is evident in her willingness to learn complex scores in order to supervise her daughters' practice sessions. Yet music is also an area in which she exerts a great deal of economic and social clout by securing for Sophia and Lulu distinguished teachers and prestigious venues in Europe as well as in New York City. Chua's status anxiety and snobbery are also hilariously evident in her disdain for the pet rabbits that she considered fat, lazy, and stupid. She made sure that the fancy dogs she eventually acquired came from a breed noted for its beauty, elegance, and preternatural intelligence. Of course, Chua was dead set on helping her Samoyeds maximize their natural cognitive gifts, since in her social context intelligence is the basis of status.    I believe that many readers find Chua annoying because her concern with status fuels their own feelings of social competitiveness. When reading her book, they may begrudgingly wonder if what is good enough for her should be good enough for them. While Americans need to raise the intellectual standards that we set for all of our children, we must also be aware of the extent to which education and the arts are often used as status weapons. Unfortunately, when weak academic standards and undisciplined behavior are cultural norms, intelligence and education become significant social markers for the educated upper middle class. Chua knows this -- if only on an intuitive level -- and she capitalizes on it.

Had I known the plot without witnessing it first-hand, I probably wouldn't have bothered checking it out.    Carrey is just plain hilarious. You can tell he's matured, but he needed to be a little older and a little more grounded for this role. He's still got his patented insanity, but in this case - he plays it off as cunning, coniving and downright manipulative. Almost scary at times, but the more light-hearted moments really get to you.     I suppose if you wanted to characterize it as one, this could be a straight up romance. I wouldn't go that far, but I'm sure others would. To me, it was a fantastic tale of a con-man on the lam, fighting to break out of jail again and again to get to whom he loves.    At the end of the day, don't let the homophobic reviews scare you - it's not all that in your face, and it's all in good fun.     Easily, 5/5.

Lots of faces lack detail and show edge enhancement throughout the film. This is a low-budget film, so I won't completely fault Media Blasters, but there does appear to be some tweaking done as the DVD did not display these problems as noticeably. At other times, though, it does seem good.<br /><br />I can't comment on the audio as I don't have a DTS-HD compatible system, but the disc does contain English DTS-HD 2.0 / 5.1 and Japanese DTS-HD 2.0 / 5.1 according to the box (it's only listed as 2.0 or 5.1 on the menu, so I can't confirm it's DTS-HD).<br /><br />I like the movie - I got the Blu-Ray cheap (cheaper than I paid for the DVD, even) so I don't regret it. 0525951113|3.0|I really enjoyed reading Daniel Suarez's debut novel Daemon. As others have noted, it has that Michael Crichton vibe and attention to detail. Here's the plot in a nutshell. Brilliant MMORPG (Massively Multi-player Online Role Playing Game) programer and company owner Matthew Sobol dies from a long battle with brain cancer. This event sets off a computer daemon (pre-programed virtual intelligence) which manipulates computer controlled devices in the real world to kill programers from Sobols company that might have been able to stop it. The Daemon then goes on to effect things in the real world when triggered by specific stories in the news. Apparently Sobol thought that society needed fixing. Before his death, he set in motion all of the key elements to make his vision happen.<br /><br />Sound a bit complicated? Indeed it is. All of the pre-planning for every contingency to make something on this scale work is mind boggling. The time, resources and money to set all of this in place makes it's likelihood improbable at best. Looking back at the story after the fact, it just seems like too much suspension of disbelief. I felt this book was intended and presented as a techno-thriller when perhaps it was really more sci-fi. Suarez includes such a large cast of characters that I was left wondering who was who at times, and I found this a bit distracting. It also left a number of key characters undeveloped. He could probably had done more with a leaner cast, but I get that he was trying to impress a sense of scale upon the reader. I was also able to predict a major plot point early on that was supposed to be a big reveal at the end, so I was a bit underwhelmed when it finally happened.<br /><br />All that didn't really matter when I was reading though. Suarez keeps the pace fast and knows his cyber-stuff. You'll learn alot about AI technology from its pages. The players range from the media, to the local cops, to the FBI, to shadowy government organizations, to corrupt big businesses. Locals center initially in California but then cover the USA and cross-cross the globe. And that is just in the physical world -- there's a lot going on in the virtual one as well. Suarez deftly ties it all together and makes fantastical technological concepts seem plausible. I would certainly recommend this book to fans of Michael Crichton and even William Gibson. It is the story of George Milton and Lennie Small, two workers who travel to farms as seasonal workers. The first of these unnumbered chapters describe their characters. George is small, thin, and quick. Lennie is big and dumb and controlled by George to keep him out of trouble. The next chapter tells about the ranch that hires them, and the conditions there. Hardly any of the migrant workers travel together. The others speak about conditions. Should Candy shoot his dog because its old and weak? Carlsson offers to do it. [A worker could own a pistol then.] The conversations tell about their lives. George dreams of owning his own ranch and working for himself. Candy offers to buy in to become their partner; he doesn't want to end up in the poor house. Curley attacks Lennie until Lennie ends the fight. Was there an accident? Saturday night most of the men go into town. Lennie talks to Crooks, the stable hand. Then to Candy. Curley's wife wanders in, and threatens them. The fifth chapter tells of Lennie's problem: he loves small animals to death. Curley's wife visits to talk. She too has dreams of a better life. Lennie begins to stroke her hair, she objects, and Lennie puts his other hand over her face. Did Lennie do a bad thing again? Candy find her on the ground. They will notify the police and hunt for Lennie.    The last chapter has the ending. Lennie dreams of Aunt Clara and a giant rabbit. Both blame him. George finds him, and they talk of their hoped-for future. There is a pistol shot. Slim says George had to do it; and they walk away for a drink. [No mention of what happened once the police investigated. I suspect this death would be covered up to avoid publicity. Just another crazy tramp?] This short story provides the background of migrant workers. They can only own what they can carry on their back. They travel from farm to farm to help with the harvest. Was Steinbeck advocating death for the old, the feeble-minded, and social misfits? Michael Crichton's \\"State of Fear\\" explains this in \\"Appendix I\\". The 1939 movie was often shown on TV in the 1950s and 1960s. Compare it to the original novel.

0670022411|5.0|Debut novelist Deborah Harkness has given us an extraordinary book, \\"A Discovery of Witches.\\" In a complicated plot of boy meets girl whereupon they fall in love.  Add to that the complication of different species, vampire vs. witch where the introduction of such couplings is outlawed by the two communities. On top of it add the separate but equal academic creds and loving of polarized families, and you have enough conflict-derived tension to keep you on the edge of your chair for the length of \\"A Discovery of Witches.\\"<br /><br />As imaginatively conceived as the complex plot, even more masterful is author Harkness's development of characterization. Diana Bishop, a bright young history professor, has built a respectable academic career as a researcher. Although she comes from a lineage of witches, she has fought with those tendencies within herself (e.g., she doesn't want to feel she's using her innate witch abilities to climb academic ladders) and uses physical activities (running, rowing, riding, etc. to repress her growing \\"witchiness.\\" To that end she has also refused family education for creating spells and protecting herself. In this novel, we watch her grow and accept herself and her powers and the two of them face the evil congregation.<br /><br />The plot is jump-started when she meets Matthew Clairmont, a strong, handsome, centuries-old vampire of superior academic achievement. The core of this drama is an ancient documents that appear to have had a spell cast upon it. The disappearance of the document becomes a cause celebre for this new \\"third world,\\" daemons, witches, and vampires who gather in the library in hopes of seizing the antique manuscript.<br /><br />In yet another arena for the action, author Harkness has invented a congregation of senior lawmakers for witches, vampires and demons. Among their rules, this organization will assassinate those who intermarry among species. For her burgeoning love affair with a vampire, Diana Bishop and Matthew Clairmont are marked for liquidation.<br /><br />As these two individuals, Diana and Matthew, try to work their way out of these deadly complications - and moving back and forth between their families' homes, one housing witches, the other housing vampires. Although \\"A Discovery of Vampires\\" is a big book, every page beginning to end is chuck full of excitement and energy as these two unlikely individuals and their just as unique communities walk a tight rope of danger to solve their problems.<br /><br />It's a measure of her consummate skill that Deborah Harkness deftly handles this big book with so many twists and turns, a book where we, the readers, are studying the facts of \\"vampirism\\" and \\"witchism\\" to understand how these very real lovable species of homo sapiens can cope and find happiness. Like all genuinely great novels, \\"A Discovery of Witches\\" educates us about lives we would never know. Author Harkness does this by writing in a style that \\"feels\\" reportorial, a factual report without emboidery - simply assumes we all accept vampires and witches and with that \\"matter of fact\\" style, we are drawn into the drama - and easily suspend incredulity. I recommend that all adult voluntary readers read Deborah Harkness's \\"A Discovery of Witches.\\"for liquidation.
